# Building on Borrowed Intelligence

### How to Start, Fund, and Defend a Company in the LLM and Agentic-AI Design Space

*A practical, researched guide for A serious would-be founder — technical or commercial — deciding whether and how to build a venture-scale or durable product on top of large language models and AI agents, who needs decision-grade analysis rather than motivational startup filler.*

---

## Introduction

All key claims are grounded. Here is the Introduction, returned as the deliverable.

---

### The Premise: You Are Building on Someone Else's Machine

Every company in this book has the same landlord. Whether you are fine-tuning an open-weight model or calling a frontier API, the core capability your product depends on was trained by OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, Meta, or a handful of others, and it improves — or reprices, or deprecates — on their schedule, not yours. That is the central fact of building in the LLM and agentic-AI design space, and it is the reason this book exists. You are building on borrowed intelligence. The intelligence is real, it is astonishing, and it is not yours. The entire discipline of starting, funding, and defending a company here is the discipline of turning a rented capability into something durable that you own.

This is not a pessimistic framing. It is a clarifying one. The founders who are winning right now understand precisely what they are renting and precisely what they are building on top of it. The founders filling the failure column mistook access to a powerful model for a business.

### Who This Book Is For

This book is written for a serious would-be founder — technical or commercial — who is deciding whether and how to build a venture-scale or durably profitable company on top of large language models and AI agents. You might be an engineer who can ship an agent over a weekend and is now asking whether the thing you can build is a thing anyone will pay for and defend. You might be a domain expert — a lawyer, a clinician, an operations leader — who sees a workflow ripe for automation and needs to understand the technical and economic realities before you commit years to it. You might be a product or commercial operator weighing whether to leave a stable role for this.

What you have in common is that you need decision-grade analysis, not motivation. There is no shortage of writing that tells you AI is the biggest opportunity of your lifetime and that you should seize it. That writing is not wrong, exactly, and it is also useless for making a decision. This book assumes you are past the point of needing to be excited and at the point of needing to be right — about where value is accruing, what actually defends a position, what the unit economics really look like, and what will kill you if you ignore it. If you want encouragement, it is easy to find elsewhere. If you want a clear-eyed map of the terrain, keep reading.

### Why Now

The urgency is not manufactured. The AI application landscape in 2026 is being shaped by a set of developments concrete enough to build a thesis around.

Capital has concentrated to a degree with few precedents. In the first quarter of 2026, AI companies captured roughly 80% of all global venture dollars — more than four of every five — according to Crunchbase, with a small cluster of foundation-model labs absorbing the majority of that. In the United States, AI's share of venture funding ran even higher through the first half of the year. This is both an opportunity and a warning: capital this concentrated inflates valuations, distorts hiring markets, and raises the bar for what "a real business" has to prove.

Adoption is moving as fast as the money. Gartner projects that the share of enterprise applications embedding task-specific AI agents will rise from under 5% in 2025 toward 40% by the end of 2026 — a step-change in enterprise willingness to let software act, not just assist. But the same analysts who forecast that surge also predict that more than 40% of agentic-AI projects will be cancelled by the end of 2027, driven not by the technology failing but by escalating costs, unclear business value, and inadequate controls. Both numbers are true at once. Demand is real and the cancellation rate is real, and the gap between them is exactly where founders win or lose.

The economics have also become legible in a way they were not eighteen months ago. AI-native companies now carry inference as a cost of goods sold, and the data has arrived: Bessemer and ICONIQ's 2026 benchmarks put AI gross margins in the 50–60% range — ICONIQ's surveyed scaling-stage average near 52% — against the 80–90% that defined classic SaaS. And the market has already sorted winners from wreckage in public view: Anysphere's Cursor crossed roughly $100M ARR in January 2025 and scaled into the billions; Harvey crossed $100M ARR by August 2025 en route to an $11B valuation. On the other side, Jasper — an early generative-AI darling valued at $1.5B in October 2022, one month before ChatGPT launched — saw revenue fall from a peak near $120M to roughly $55M and cut its internal valuation. Now is the moment to build because the patterns are finally observable. You do not have to guess anymore.

### What This Book Will — and Won't — Tell You

This book will give you frameworks and current data to answer the questions that actually determine outcomes: where in the stack value is concentrating; whether your idea has a moat or is renting one; how to price when cost scales with usage rather than seats; how to model unit economics honestly; how architecture choices become commercial ones; why evaluation is now a business function; what the EU AI Act and liability landscape demand; how to hire, and how to raise into a distorted market. It will ground every principle in dated, real company histories rather than abstractions.

It will not tell you which specific product to build — that is your thesis to form, and Chapter 1 is about forming it well rather than handing you an answer. It will not teach you to build a model; this is a book about building a company on top of models. It will not promise that following it guarantees success — the base rate for AI application startups is unforgiving, and pretending otherwise would defeat the purpose. And because this landscape moves fast, specific figures here are timestamped; treat the frameworks as durable and the numbers as snapshots to re-check. Where the data is contested or the outcome genuinely unknown — such as who is liable when an agent errs — this book says so rather than faking certainty.

### How to Use This Book

The chapters are sequenced as a founder's decision path: from reading the stack and locating a defensible wedge, through business model, unit economics, and architecture, into the operational disciplines of evaluation and go-to-market, then the legal, team, and fundraising terrain, and finally a concrete launch playbook for the first 12–18 months. Read straight through if you are early. If you are already building, jump to your live question — each chapter stands alone — but return to the moat and unit-economics chapters, because those are the two that most often quietly decide whether the rest matters. Keep a running document of your own answers as you go. By the end you should hold not a feeling but a thesis: what you are building, why it defends, what it costs to run, and what could kill it. That is the deliverable. Borrowed intelligence can still build something that is unmistakably yours — if you go in knowing exactly what you have borrowed.

---

## 1. The Opportunity Landscape: Reading the Stack Before You Bet

I have what I need. The three superlatives resolve to different definitions: Cursor is fastest **$1M→$100M** (~12 months, Sacra, prosumer/self-serve), Lovable is fastest **from launch** (~8 months, self-reported, consumer app-builder), and Legora is fastest **enterprise** business (~18 months, Bessemer). Here is the fully corrected chapter.

---

### The Stack Is Not a Metaphor — It's a Map of Where Money Lands

Before you decide what to build, you need a physical picture of the system you're building on, because value is not distributed evenly across it and it is actively moving. The LLM/agent stack has four layers, and each has a different economic logic. One caution before we start: almost every framework in this chapter comes from a venture firm talking its own book — a16z, Bessemer, Foundation Capital, and Euclid all hold positions in the companies their reports rank or celebrate. The data is usable; the framing is interested. Read accordingly.

**Foundation-model labs** sit at the bottom: OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, xAI, Meta. This is where capital is most brutally concentrated. In Q1 2026, global venture funding hit an all-time record of roughly $300B across ~6,000 startups, and AI companies captured $242B of it — about 80%, up from AI's prior record of 55% a year earlier [Source: news.crunchbase.com/venture/record-breaking-funding-ai-global-q1-2026]. Four rounds — OpenAI ($122B), Anthropic ($30B), xAI ($20B), Waymo ($16B) — totaled $188B, which Crunchbase reports as **65% of *all* global venture dollars** that quarter [Source: same]. (Treat the $122B single-round OpenAI figure with caution; verify against primary SoftBank/OpenAI disclosures before repeating it.)

Revenue is scaling to match, and the competitive picture already flipped. In **April 2026 Anthropic passed OpenAI in reported annualized revenue** — roughly $30B run-rate versus OpenAI's ~$25B — months earlier than Epoch AI's central estimate, which had penciled the crossover in around August 2026 [Source: epoch.ai/data-insights/anthropic-openai-revenue; the-ai-corner.com/p/anthropic-30b-arr-passed-openai-revenue-2026]. Two reality checks belong next to that headline. First, OpenAI disputes Anthropic's gross-revenue accounting; an April 2026 internal memo argued the comparable *net* figure is closer to ~$22B — even the topline numbers are contested. Second, the eye-watering growth multiples are cooling: both labs' internal 2026 plans reportedly assume far slower growth than the 3–10x of 2025 (on the order of ~2x for OpenAI and ~4x for Anthropic). The trend lines flatter the layer more than the operators expect.

**The takeaway for a founder: you are not building at this layer.** Crunchbase's own concentration analysis shows late-stage AI dollars pooling in a handful of names [Source: news.crunchbase.com/venture/capital-concentrated-ai-global-q1-2026]. A small number of labs — plus self-funding giants like Google and Meta — will absorb essentially all of the capital, talent, and compute here. Your relationship to them is as a customer and a hostage, not a competitor. (We will get to the mitigation for "hostage.")

**Infrastructure, orchestration, and observability** is the middle layer: the plumbing that turns a model API into a running agent — orchestration (Temporal, Inngest, LangGraph), observability and eval (Braintrust, LangSmith, Helicone, the recently-acquired Langfuse), and gateways (LiteLLM). This layer is where the froth-to-revenue gap is most visible. LangChain raised $125M at a $1.25B valuation in October 2025 — becoming a unicorn — on reported ARR of only ~$12–16M [Source: techcrunch.com/2025/10/21/open-source-agentic-startup-langchain-hits-1-25b-valuation]. That is a **~78–104x multiple** (depending on where in the range ARR actually sat) on the *promise* of the layer, priced while the labs give away competing tooling for free.

**The horizontal application layer** — general-purpose tools sold across every industry — is where startups actually collect the most dollars from other startups. a16z's October 2025 spending report (built on Mercury bank data) found horizontal applications were 60% of the top-50 products startups pay for, with OpenAI (#1), Anthropic (#2), and Replit (#3) topping the list; creative tools were the single largest category, with ten ranked companies [Source: a16z.com/the-ai-application-spending-report-where-startup-dollars-really-go]. Nearly 70% of the list doesn't require enterprise licenses — bottom-up, individual adoption drives the expansion.

**The vertical-agent layer** — deep, industry-specific systems (legal, healthcare, insurance) — is where deal *count* and the most credible durable businesses are forming. Euclid Ventures' Vertical Report 2026 found verticals captured 53% of 2025 deal volume but only 30% of capital ($56.2B vs $129.8B), precisely because model/infra mega-rounds skew the dollar totals; excluding mega-rounds, verticals took 51% of capital, and at the earliest ($1–5M) stage they reached 60% share by Q4 2025 [Source: insights.euclid.vc/p/the-vertical-report-2026-full-version].

Hold those two facts side by side — horizontals win headline spend and bottom-up distribution, verticals win deal count and depth — because the tension between them is the central strategic question of this chapter.

### What the Labs Eat, and What They Can't Reach

The single most important structural fact for a founder is that the foundation-model labs have a panoramic, real-time view of what's working — they can see it in API usage — and they move up the stack on their own cadence. In Silicon Valley this is called getting *"Sherlocked"* (an Apple-ecosystem term for when the platform owner ships your feature into the OS) — and it has already happened twice.

The 2022 *"wrapper era"* — thin prompt-UIs over a model — was eaten the moment the labs shipped their own interfaces. **Jasper AI**, an AI-writing tool that raised $125M at a ~$1.5B valuation in October 2022, is the canonical casualty: its value proposition was a friendlier UI over GPT-3, and when ChatGPT launched a month later, buyers stopped paying $49/month for what the OpenAI playground did for free. Revenue fell from a reported peak near ~$120M toward ~$55M, Jasper cut its internal valuation to ~$1.2B, and it churned through multiple CEOs and layoffs [Source: maginative.com/article/jasper-cuts-internal-valuation-as-ai-growth-slows]. The same dynamic recurs at the feature level: when OpenAI shipped ChatGPT Connectors and native file/tool integrations, it absorbed a swath of standalone "connect-your-data" startups whose entire product was one integration.

The orchestration layer is being commoditized *right now*, from above, by the labs themselves. At DevDay on October 6, 2025, OpenAI shipped **AgentKit / Agent Builder** — a visual drag-and-drop multi-agent canvas Sam Altman called *"like Canva for building agents"* [Source: techcrunch.com/2025/10/06/openai-launches-agentkit-to-help-developers-build-and-ship-ai-agents; cnbc.com/2025/10/06/open-ai-devday-live-updates-altman-jony-ive]. Anthropic's Model Context Protocol (MCP), published in late 2024, became the de facto tool-integration standard within about a year. Both the OpenAI Agents SDK and the Claude Agent SDK now compete directly with independent frameworks. This is why LangChain's ~80–100x valuation-to-revenue gap should scare you rather than inspire you: the middle of the stack is being given away by the people who own the layer beneath it.

Observability, eval, and memory are the *plausible next targets* — a forecast, not a fact, but a well-grounded one: OpenAI already ships evals and tracing inside its platform, and Langfuse's January 2026 acquisition by ClickHouse (inside a $400M Series D at a $15B valuation) is what consolidation of that sub-layer looks like in real time [Source: clickhouse.com/blog/clickhouse-raises-400-million-series-d-acquires-langfuse-launches-postgres; siliconangle.com/2026/01/16/database-maker-clickhouse-raises-400m-acquires-ai-observability-startup-langfuse].

Now the useful part. Foundation Capital names five *structural protections* — properties that make an agentic business resistant to being eaten, each with a named example [Source: foundationcapital.com/ideas/when-model-providers-eat-everything]:

1. **Master high-exception workflows** — where ambiguous cases need human-in-the-loop judgment (Fulcrum, in insurance claims). Labs optimize for the general case; the exceptions are where domain value lives.
2. **Own proprietary feedback loops in fuzzy-ground-truth domains** — where "correct" isn't in the training data (PlayerZero for distributed-systems debugging, Traversal for incident response).
3. **Own the execution layer in fragmented, legacy stacks** — the gritty integration work labs won't do (Maximor building a "shadow ERP," Optimized on DocuSign).
4. **Hire domain experts and embed with customers** — ex-practitioners sitting alongside users (Regie, with former sales-engagement execs sitting with SDRs and RevOps).
5. **Be fast and stay in the details.**

The unifying moat, in Foundation Capital's words: *"the stream of interaction data generated by users and agents working in tandem is becoming the key proprietary asset in AI."* A model can be copied; the exhaust from thousands of real, human-corrected workflow runs in a specific domain cannot be, because the labs don't have access to it. a16z frames the same defensibility from the buyer's side: durable wedges require deep, customer-specific integration that is *"gritty, in-the-weeds"* — Tennr's healthcare fax integrations, HappyRobot's connections into trucking TMS systems [Source: a16z.com/insights-for-enterprise-ai-builders]. The ugliness is the moat. That is the author's heuristic, not a law: if your integration is elegant and generic, assume a lab can reproduce it; if it is legacy-specific and hated, assume they won't bother.

### Sizing a Real Problem: The Difference Between a Budget and a Demo

The hardest discipline in this space is telling a *budgeted, urgent* problem apart from a *demo-impressive, nobody-pays* one — and the evidence that most founders fail this test is overwhelming. The MIT NANDA "GenAI Divide" study (150 leader interviews, a 350-employee survey, 300 public deployments) found that **roughly 95% of enterprise generative-AI pilots delivered no measurable P&L impact**; only ~5% achieved rapid revenue acceleration [Source: fortune.com/2025/08/18/mit-report-95-percent-generative-ai-pilots-at-companies-failing-cfo]. The root cause was not model quality — it was a *"learning gap"*: the tools didn't adapt to the workflow. Two findings should reshape how you pick a problem:

- **Buy beats build ~2x.** Buying from a specialized vendor succeeded ~67% of the time versus ~33% for internal builds. This is your permission to exist — but only if you're the specialist, not another generic platform the customer could assemble themselves.
- **The money is aimed at the wrong problems.** More than 50% of GenAI budgets went to sales and marketing, but the biggest realized ROI was in back-office automation — eliminating BPO spend, cutting agency costs. The unglamorous middle- and back-office is under-served relative to its budget.

**Now do the arithmetic the demo never makes you do.** A "sizing" is not a TAM slide; it is a line item you can quote. Take a claims-processing operation run by 200 offshore agents at a ~$40K fully-loaded annual cost — that is ~$8M of addressable manual spend sitting in one budget owner's P&L. If you can absorb 60% of that volume at 30% of the cost, the honest contract-value story is roughly $1.4M ACV, anchored to a real line item someone is *already* paying. Compare that to the shape of the MIT 95%: a $75K "innovation budget" pilot owned by a team with no P&L, which evaporates at the next reforecast. The test is not "would this be useful" — it is "whose existing budget line shrinks when we win, and does that person control it." A real budget owner is a VP of operations quoting headcount and BPO invoices; the trap is an innovation team quoting curiosity.

So how do you positively identify a real problem? a16z's framework is the sharpest available [Source: a16z.com/insights-for-enterprise-ai-builders]:

1. **Sell the work output, not software to help people work.** When you sell a completed deposition summary or a resolved support ticket, you draw on *labor* budgets, which dwarf software budgets. This is the "service-as-software" shift.
2. **Buyers pull, they don't get pushed.** Real demand shows up as inbound; you're managing a waitlist, not chasing a pipeline.
3. **CEO-led mandates with dedicated AI budgets.**
4. **Speed to revenue.** Stripe's 2025 annual letter found the top AI companies on its platform reached $5M in annualized revenue in a median of ~24 months, versus ~37 months for the leading SaaS cohort of the prior generation — a real budgeted wedge shows up in revenue velocity, not just usage graphs [Source: stripe.com/blog/inside-the-growth-of-the-top-ai-companies-on-stripe].

The counter-example is the discipline's spine — but tell it correctly. **Builder.ai** — a "$1.5B AI app builder" backed by Microsoft, SoftBank, and Qatar's QIA — entered insolvency in 2025. The viral version of the story ("its Natasha AI was really ~700 human engineers pretending to be AI") was **largely debunked**: Natasha was a genuine LLM-based code generator, and the outsourced developers were doing normal client app-delivery work [Source: blog.pragmaticengineer.com/builder-ai-did-not-fake-ai]. The real scandal was *financial*, and it is the sharper lesson: revenue was allegedly round-tripped with Indian firm VerSe (each billing the other for roughly matching amounts), forcing restatements from a claimed ~$220M of 2024 revenue down to ~$55M, and 2023 from ~$180M to ~$45M; the company owed ~$85M to AWS and ~$30M to Microsoft and had gone ~10 months without a CFO [Source: bloomberg.com/news/articles/2025-05-30/builder-ai-faked-business-with-indian-firm-verse-to-inflate-sales-sources-say; sifted.eu/articles/builder-ai-verse-innovation-daily-hunt-faked-business-bloomberg]. (VerSe denies the round-tripping characterization.) The demo-vs-business gap here wasn't fake AI — it was **fake revenue**, which is exactly what "demo-impressive, nobody-pays" looks like when it's dressed up for investors. The subtler version is legal: a16z's data shows Replit generated ~15x more revenue than Lovable among Mercury customers *despite* Lovable's higher consumer traffic. Consumer buzz is not durable revenue.

### "Vertical AI Eats Horizontal SaaS": The Evidence, and Where It Breaks

The bull case is real and clusters in exactly the domains theory predicts — legal, healthcare, financial services — where proprietary workflow data is inaccessible to the labs and the regulatory surface is thick.

- **Legora** (Swedish legal AI) hit **$100M ARR in ~18 months**, which Bessemer called the fastest *enterprise* business ever to $100M ARR, and raised a $550M Series D at a $5.55B valuation [Source: bvp.com/atlas/legora-the-fastest-enterprise-business-to-reach-100m-arr]. Disclosure: Bessemer is a Legora investor. A word on that superlative, because this book quotes three different "fastest to $100M ARR" records and a careful reader will notice they can't all be first: they are measuring different races. Legora's is the fastest *enterprise* (top-down, seat-and-contract) business. In the consumer/self-serve column, Cursor is described by Sacra as the fastest SaaS company ever to go **from $1M to $100M ARR** (~12 months) — a different clock, since it starts at $1M, not launch — and Lovable *self-reports* the fastest run **from launch to $100M** (~8 months), an unaudited vendor claim about a consumer app-builder. Keep the qualifiers attached whenever you cite any of them; stripped of "enterprise," "$1M-to-$100M," or "self-reported from launch," all three collapse into a contradiction that only exists on the book jacket. (Cursor and Lovable are examined in the competitive-landscape and value-and-moats chapters respectively.)
- **Harvey** (legal) reached ~$300M ARR by May 2026 (up from ~$195M at end-2025) and closed a $200M round at an $11B valuation in March 2026 [Source: sacra.com/c/harvey; forbes.com/sites/iainmartin/2026/02/09/legal-ai-startup-harvey-in-talks-to-raise-200-million-at-11-billion-valuation].
- **OpenEvidence** ("ChatGPT for doctors") topped **$100M in annual revenue** and doubled its valuation to $12B in a $250M January 2026 Series D led by Thrive and DST [Source: cnbc.com/2026/01/21/openevidence-chatgpt-for-doctors-doubles-valuation-to-12-billion].
- **Abridge** (ambient clinical) reached **150+ health systems** and a $5.3B valuation in a June 2025 Series E led by a16z, on contracted ARR reported around ~$117M [Source: fiercehealthcare.com/ai-and-machine-learning/ambient-ai-startup-abridge-scores-300m-series-e-backed-a16z-and-khosla].
- **Sierra** (Bret Taylor's customer-service agents) hit **$200M ARR by May 2026** and raised a $950M Series E at a $15.8B valuation led by Tiger Global and GV [Source: finance.yahoo.com/markets/stocks/articles/sierra-raises-950m-15-8b-154125641; axios.com/pro/enterprise-software-deals/2026/05/04/sierra-ai-valuation-agentic-enterprise]. Note the category caveat: Sierra sells customer service *across* industries. It is a **function-vertical** — deep in one workflow rather than one industry — which counts for the "depth beats breadth" thesis, but not for "industry-specific" in the strict sense; the chapter's taxonomy has to stretch to include it.

On the demand side, Gartner predicts 40% of enterprise applications will feature task-specific AI agents by end of 2026, up from less than 5% in 2025 [Source: gartner.com/en/newsroom/press-releases/2025-08-26-gartner-predicts-40-percent-of-enterprise-apps-will-feature-task-specific-ai-agents-by-2026]. But cite that number with its sibling from the same house: Gartner also predicts **over 40% of agentic-AI projects will be canceled by end of 2027** on escalating costs, unclear value, and inadequate risk controls, and estimates only ~130 of thousands of "agentic" vendors are real rather than "agent-washed" [Source: gartner.com/en/newsroom/press-releases/2025-06-25-gartner-predicts-over-40-percent-of-agentic-ai-projects-will-be-canceled-by-end-of-2027]. Adoption and mortality are the same coin.

Now, honestly, **where the thesis breaks down** [Source: insights.euclid.vc/p/the-vertical-report-2026-full-version]:

- **Verticals win only ~45% of $100M+ mega-rounds** — model and infra capture the rest.
- **A growth-stage "graduation bottleneck."** Verticals are strong at seed but plateau at Series A/B; seeds are now priced like prior-cycle Series As.
- **Exit dependency on private equity.** Over 80% of PE-backed software acquisitions target verticals, against a thin IPO pipeline (18 vertical IPOs, $4.3B aggregate, in 2025). Plan for a PE exit, not a public one.
- **Horizontals are getting better at surface-level tasks**, forcing verticals toward genuine depth — the shallow vertical wrapper is as exposed as the horizontal one.
- **The labs are already circling.** Euclid warns that *"OpenAI and Anthropic have already made overtures"* in Legal and Finance.

And the valuations demand skepticism. Sierra's $15.8B on ~$200M ARR is ~79x; LangChain ~80–100x. These are mean-reversion candidates, not guarantees — and the visible winners are *survivors*; the MIT 95%-failure rate and Builder.ai are the counterweight that keeps this from being a highlight reel.

**The occupied-vertical problem.** Notice that two of the marquee wins — Harvey ($300M ARR, $11B) and Legora ($100M ARR, $5.55B) — are in the *same* vertical. That is not open territory; that is a forming duopoly, and the funding data says the window in flagship verticals is closing fast. If you are staring at "legal AI" or "ambient clinical scribing" in 2026, the honest advice is: don't enter the category, enter the *gap in it*. The wedges that remain are sub-vertical (not "legal" but immigration filings, or municipal-bond disclosure, or insurance-defense billing review), geographic (Legora won Europe before the US incumbents noticed), or segment-based (AmLaw-100 tooling is spoken for; the mid-market and solo/small-firm long tail is not). A generic assault on an occupied vertical is the fastest way to become a line in next year's failure count.

Reconciling the tension with a16z's "horizontals are 60% of spend" data: **horizontals win breadth, distribution, and near-term dollars; verticals win depth, defensibility, and deal count.** The horizontal opportunity is largely spoken for by the labs and a few distribution-heavy incumbents. The vertical opportunity is where an *outsider founder with domain access* can still win — which is why "three of five new venture-backed companies are building for a specific industry."

### The Unit Economics of the Bet — Before You Read the Signals

Layer choice is a margin choice, and founders skip this at their peril. **Infra/tooling** carries near-software gross margins but sits directly under the labs' free tooling — good margins, terrible durability. **Horizontal apps** can be high-margin but compete with the model provider's own consumer product. **Vertical / "service-as-software" agents** make the opposite trade: they reach into labor budgets ten times the size of software budgets, but they carry *services-like* gross margins, because every output eats inference cost *plus* the human-in-the-loop review labor that constitutes the moat. You are not selling 85%-margin SaaS; you are selling something closer to a high-40s-to-60s-margin automated service that improves as the HITL cost curve bends down with each corrected run. Budget for that review labor as COGS, not as a temporary crutch.

That dictates **pricing**. "Sell the work output" means pricing per unit of resolved work — per resolved ticket (Sierra's outcomes model), per document, per claim, per summary — not per seat. The failure mode is specific and worth stating plainly: outcome pricing deflates when the model improves and the task gets cheap to perform, so a naive per-resolution price can collapse your revenue exactly as your product gets better. Price against the *labor you replace* (a fraction of the loaded human cost), not against your marginal compute cost, and re-index as models improve.

And plan the **hostage mitigation** into the architecture from day one, because you are dependent on the labs. Route across multiple models behind a gateway; keep an eval harness rich enough that switching providers is a config change, not a rewrite; hold your proprietary interaction data and evals as *your* asset, not the lab's; and — Foundation Capital's own point — avoid standardizing your product on a single rival lab's SDK, which hands your switching cost to a company that may compete with you next quarter. Model-agnosticism has a real engineering cost; in a two-supplier market moving this fast, it is usually worth it.

Finally, the **regulatory surface is both the moat and the bill.** HIPAA in healthcare, privilege and confidentiality in legal — these are precisely *why* the labs stay out of the last mile, which is what protects Abridge and Harvey. But crossing that surface is not free: SOC 2, BAAs, clinical or legal validation, and enterprise procurement cycles measured in quarters are the price of the moat. If you're not resourced to pay it, the protected domain isn't protected *for you*.

### The Wedge Signals Worth a Multi-Year Commitment

Reading the stack tells you where *not* to go: not the model layer (capital-locked), not naked orchestration (given away by the labs), not thin wrappers (Sherlocked on the next release), not an occupied vertical's front door. It points toward a deep vertical or function-vertical agent in a data-rich, exception-heavy, budget-backed domain. These are qualitative gates, not a scored rubric — a candidate that clears most of them honestly is worth years of your life; one carried mostly by a demo and a TAM slide is the thing 95% of pilots turned out to be:

1. **Design-partner pull, not push.** Be concrete about the threshold: at seed, "real" is two or three *named* customers on **paid pilots** (not LOIs) at contracts large enough to imply a production budget — think five-to-low-six figures, not a $10K trial. A champion with a mandate can name the budget line and the executive behind it; an enthusiast can only name the cool demo.
2. **Existing manual spend you can point at.** The problem is currently a BPO contract, an agency retainer, or a team of contractors — a labor line-item you can quote (headcount × loaded cost × addressable share), not a vague "productivity" story.
3. **Workflow pain concentrated in high-exception cases.** The value is in the 20% of cases that break the general model — where your human-in-the-loop loop compounds into proprietary data the labs can't reach.
4. **Gritty, defensible integration.** Legacy connections (fax, TMS, a specific ERP, DocuSign) ugly enough that no lab will bother reproducing them.
5. **Domain expertise on the founding team.** You (or a co-founder) have sat in the seat — Foundation Capital's protection #4, and the one you can't hire your way into after the fact.
6. **Revenue velocity once you start.** If it's real and budgeted, it should track toward the Stripe $5M-ARR benchmark; if usage is high but revenue is flat, you may have a Lovable, not a Replit.

The point of reading the stack before you bet is not to find the exciting layer — it's to find the boring, defensible, budgeted one, in the gap the incumbents haven't reached, that the labs will never bother to eat.

---

## 2. Where the Moat Actually Is: Beyond the 'Thin Wrapper' Critique

All key facts are verified. Here is the revised chapter.

### The Autopsy Comes First: What Actually Killed Jasper

In October 2022, Jasper raised $125 million at a $1.5 billion valuation, on roughly $80 million of ARR and internal projections of $250 million by the end of 2024 [Source: Contrary Research, research.contrary.com/company/jasper]. Six weeks later, OpenAI launched ChatGPT. By summer 2023 Jasper had cut its revenue forecast by at least 30% (per The Information); by September 2023 it had cut its own internal valuation ~20% to about $1.2 billion, and founder-CEO David Rogenmoser had handed the company to ex-Dropbox president Timothy Young [Source: Contrary Research; Maginative]. Later teardowns circulate figures like "$120M to $55M ARR" — treat those as unverified secondary estimates — but the direction and the cause are not in dispute.

The cause matters more than the numbers. Jasper did not lose to a competitor with a better product. It lost because its core value proposition — access to GPT-3, mediated through templates and prompts for marketing copy — was given away by its own supplier, effectively free, to a hundred million people. The moment the model was directly reachable, the paid intermediary was structurally unnecessary. Jasper owned neither the model, nor the customer's workflow, nor any data the model couldn't reproduce.

This failure mode generalizes, and it hits incumbents too. Chegg's stock peaked at $113.51 in February 2021 (market cap roughly $14B–14.7B, per Forbes and MacroTrends); it issued its first ChatGPT earnings warning in May 2023 — the stock fell ~48% in a single day on that news [Source: CNBC, May 2023] — and by late 2025 the company was worth roughly $115–156 million, a ~99% decline, after cutting 45% of its workforce [Source: Forbes, Oct 2025; CNBC]. Note that a *second, distinct* ~49% figure appears in this story: in its February 2025 antitrust suit against Google, Chegg alleged its non-subscriber traffic was down 49% year-over-year as of January 2025 due to AI Overviews [Source: CNBC, Feb 24, 2025; Search Engine Land]. Do not conflate the one-day stock drop with the traffic claim — but do notice both trace to the same root: a content library that a general-purpose model can reproduce on demand is not an asset, it is inventory awaiting obsolescence.

Sam Altman said the quiet part in a 2024 20VC interview: companies that "assume the model is not going to get better and build little things on top" versus those that bet on improvement — "95% of the world should be betting on the latter... when we just do our fundamental job, we're going to steamroll you... not because we don't like you, but because we have a mission" [Source: the-decoder.com]. The dynamic he described was made concrete at OpenAI's DevDay in November 2023: the launch of custom GPTs and native retrieval commoditized, in one keynote, an entire cohort of "chat with your PDF" startups representing hundreds of millions of invested dollars [Source: Computerworld; Hacker News discussion]. The pattern recurs quarterly. Plan for it.

### The Churn Data: "AI Churns" Really Means "Cheap Horizontal AI Churns"

Before building the taxonomy, look at what retention data actually shows, because it's more nuanced than the doom narrative. ChartMogul's "AI churn wave" report is the best public dataset. The genuinely catastrophic numbers in it are these: median gross revenue retention for AI-native startups was ~27% in January 2025, and AI products priced under $50/month showed 23% GRR and 32% NRR — meaning the typical cheap AI product lost three-quarters of a revenue cohort within a year. On net retention, AI-native companies posted a median NRR of 48% against 82% for traditional B2B SaaS [Source: chartmogul.com/reports/saas-retention-the-ai-churn-wave/, verified directly].

But two findings cut against the doom reading, from the same report. First, the effect is sharply price-tier dependent: $50–249/month products showed 45% GRR and 61% NRR, while products over $250/month showed 70% GRR and 85% NRR — which the report itself describes as "essentially the same as B2B SaaS." Second, the median GRR *recovered* from that 27% in January 2025 to ~40% by September 2025 as tourist customers churned out and committed users remained.

The decision-grade reading: the market is not punishing "AI products." It is punishing cheap, horizontal, low-commitment AI products — precisely the products whose value is closest to raw model access. Where price implies embedded workflow value and organizational commitment, retention normalizes. This is the empirical outline of the moat map.

### Thin vs. Thick: A Working Definition

A product is *thin* when the user's outcome is approximately `model(prompt)` — when everything between the customer and the frontier model could be reconstructed by a competent team in weeks, or shipped by the platform as a feature. The definitive proof that prompt engineering is not a moat came from OpenAI itself: Study Mode, its tutoring feature launched July 2025 with input from 40+ institutions, was implemented essentially as a system prompt — and later quietly removed [Source: TechCrunch, July 2025; Hacker News]. If your differentiation can be shipped by the platform as a system prompt, it can also be killed by the platform as one. Prompts, templates, persona libraries, UI polish, "being first," and undifferentiated fine-tunes on public data all fail this test.

A product is *thick* when meaningful value survives the counterfactual "the base model just got 10x better and free." That value must live in one of a small number of places, examined below. Note what's *not* on the list: model quality itself. Andrew Chen's February 2025 essay "Revenge of the GPT Wrappers" makes the structural argument — state-of-the-art models stay only ~6 months ahead of open-source rivals (DeepSeek, Grok), so model superiority is a depreciating asset even for the labs; app-layer defensibility must come from the classic mechanisms that made "database wrappers" (i.e., all of Web 2.0's CRUD apps) durable: acquisition, engagement, and monetization network effects [Source: andrewchen.substack.com].

One caveat governs everything below. Several exemplars carry large valuations, and this chapter cites them — but investors buying a moat narrative is evidence of the narrative, not the moat. A $2B round "because of the data asset" is a bet that the moat holds, not proof that it does. Treat valuations as context; treat retention, switching costs, and survived platform attacks as evidence.

### The Taxonomy: Six Moats That Survive Model Progress

**1. Proprietary data flywheels — with strict preconditions.** The most cited and most oversold moat. The essential corrective is a16z's own 2019 essay "The Empty Promise of Data Moats" (Casado & Lauten): most claimed "data network effects" are actually data *scale* effects with sharply diminishing returns — the cost of acquiring incremental unique data rises while its marginal value falls, so data alone rarely defends anything [Source: a16z.com/the-empty-promise-of-data-moats/, verified directly]. A real flywheel requires four preconditions: the data is **proprietary** (not scrapable, not synthesizable by a better base model); it is **generated as exhaust from usage** (so it compounds without a separate acquisition cost); it is **tied to measurable outcome improvement**; and that improvement is **visible to customers**.

EvenUp is the cleanest live example. Its Settlement Repository aggregates anonymized settlement outcomes from 2,000+ personal-injury law firms, plus millions of medical records — data that does not exist on the open web and that every new customer enriches. The company claims (a self-reported marketing metric, without published methodology) that its demand letters are 69% more likely to hit policy-limit settlements; Bessemer cited the data moat as its investment rationale in a round valuing the company above $2B — which, per the caveat above, tells you what investors believe, not what will hold [Source: evenuplaw.com; Crunchbase News]. Still: no frontier model release reproduces settlement outcomes locked inside law firms' case files. Cursor's telemetry-trained in-house models are the same mechanism in a different domain — one secondary analysis estimates its tab/Composer models handled roughly half of autocomplete requests by late 2025, though this is a single-source figure without primary confirmation [Source: mmntm.net Cursor deep dive]. Contrast the failure mode: "we fine-tune on user chats" fails every precondition — the data is low-signal, outcome-unlinked, and the next base model makes the fine-tune irrelevant.

*Bootstrapping the flywheel.* The preconditions describe steady state; on day one you have zero exhaust. The practical sequence is: (a) recruit 3–10 design partners in exchange for pricing or exclusivity, and instrument **outcome labels from the first user session** — the settlement amount, the accepted-vs-rejected edit, the claim outcome — because usage data without outcome labels never becomes a flywheel; (b) buy or manually assemble the seed corpus (EvenUp began by processing firms' historical case files — the first increment of its repository was labor, not exhaust); (c) accept that the flywheel is a lagging moat: expect tens of customers and several quarters before pooled data measurably beats what any single customer contributes, and do not pitch the flywheel as your defense before that crossover — pitch the workflow (moat #3) that generates it.

**2. Deep integration into systems of record.** Abridge, the ambient clinical-documentation company ($5.3B valuation after its June 2025 Series E), made its decisive move not in modeling but in plumbing: a deep Epic partnership — including, per Contrary Research's analysis (one analyst's interpretation, not a disclosed contract), equity/revenue-share arrangements — giving preferential integration into the ~38% of hospital networks running Epic, with notes flowing directly into chart sections and what Contrary estimates as a 3–6 month integration lead over rivals [Source: research.contrary.com/company/abridge; Fierce Healthcare]. Harvey in legal shows the same shift: as frontier models commoditized raw legal reasoning, Harvey's defense migrated to iManage/NetDocuments/Word integration, a LexisNexis alliance embedding Shepard's citations, and workflow orchestration. At its March 2026 raise ($200M at $11B, co-led by GIC and Sequoia), Harvey's ARR was roughly $195M (end-2025 figure); Sacra estimates it reached ~$300M by May 2026, with ~50% of the Am Law 100 as customers [Source: CNBC, March 25, 2026; sacra.com/c/harvey]. The moat is around the model, not in it. A better model doesn't dissolve an Epic integration; it makes the integrated product better.

**3. Workflow lock-in and accumulated context.** The survivors of the first wrapper cohort all made the same pivot: from single-shot generation (a commodity) to owning a multi-step workflow (a switching cost). Copy.ai abandoned copywriting entirely for a "GTM AI Platform" of revenue-ops workflows and was acquired by Fullcast in late 2025; Tome shut its AI-slides product in April 2025, stating it "failed to find a sustainable path," and relaunched as Lightfield, an AI CRM [Source: averi.ai; deckary.com]. Lovable — which claims to be the fastest software company to $100M ARR, in ~8 months (a company-promoted superlative; definitions vary and Cursor and others are cited as faster on other measures) — is built entirely on third-party frontier models; its moat attempt is owning the full build-deploy-host loop and the user's accumulated project artifacts [Source: TechCrunch, July 2025 and Dec 2025 ($330M at $6.6B)]. The wrapper label predicted nothing about Lovable; workflow depth and speed did. For agentic products, accumulated context — memory, learned preferences, connected credentials, audit history — is the emerging version of this moat.

**4. Distribution and speed.** Distribution is a moat both for you and against you. Microsoft bundles Copilot into existing Microsoft 365 licensing, so enterprises adopt through contracts, admin tooling, and compliance certifications (SOC 2, ISO 27001, HIPAA, FedRAMP) already in place — a structural headwind for any startup asking Fortune 500 procurement to bet on a five-year-old company [Source: windowsforum.com; cloudwars.com]. The startup counter is vertical depth plus bottom-up speed: Cursor's developer-led motion took it past $500M ARR by June 2025 and reportedly ~$2B by early 2026 with over half the Fortune 500 [Source: TechCrunch, June 2025; TNW]. But note a contradiction you must resolve for yourself: the bottom-up, swipe-a-card playbook that built Cursor and Lovable **does not transfer** to the regulated verticals this chapter recommends. Hospitals and Am Law firms buy top-down, through committees. The motion that works there is the one Abridge and Harvey actually ran: land 2–3 lighthouse design partners via executive relationships, co-develop for months, convert pilots into multi-year contracts, then use those logos and KLAS-style rankings as the wedge into the next twenty accounts. It is slower, reference-driven, and front-loads compliance cost — budget for it. Speed itself functions as a moat only while it compounds into one of the other five; it buys time, not defense.

**5. Brand, trust, and regulatory position.** OpenEvidence — "ChatGPT for doctors," adopted by roughly 40% of US licensed physicians at ~18M clinical queries/month, valued at $12B in January 2026 — is not defensible because of its model. It is defensible because of exclusive/licensed access to NEJM, JAMA, and Cochrane content with peer-reviewed citations on every answer, plus an NPI-verified physician user base monetized at $70–150+ pharma-ad CPMs versus $5–15 typical social CPMs [Source: CNBC, Jan 2026; sacra.com/c/openevidence]. A frontier lab cannot replicate an exclusive NEJM license by shipping a better model. Similarly, Abridge's back-to-back #1 "Best in KLAS" rankings act as a procurement filter inside hospital buying committees — trust artifacts that take years to accumulate.

The whitespace is quantifiable. Anthropic's usage data shows software engineering accounting for ~49.7% of agentic tool calls on its API, while healthcare is ~1%, legal ~0.9%, and education ~1.8%; YC president Garry Tan's explicit advice, in his February 21, 2026 essay built on that data, was to stop piling into coding agents and target the untouched verticals [Source: garryslist.org, "Half the AI Agent Market Is One Category. The Rest Is Wide Open."; Anthropic Economic Index / agent usage reports, anthropic.com].

But the barrier that shields you is a toll you must pay first, and it has a price sheet. A first SOC 2 Type II runs roughly $50–100K and 6–12 months for a small company; HITRUST — required or strongly preferred by most US health systems onboarding vendors — runs $20–70K for an e1 up to $150–500K and 9–24 months for an r2; a production Epic integration typically takes 6–18 months from initiation to deployment, through security review, build, testing, certification, and launch [Source: soc2auditors.org; intuitionlabs.ai HIPAA-for-startups guide; invene.com Epic integration guide; JMIR case study]. Add FedRAMP (commonly $500K+ and a year or more) if you sell to government. These numbers, not the model, are the real seed-stage budget question in regulated verticals. And the caveat: regulatory moats decay too. Labs ship HIPAA-eligible APIs, and Epic itself is building native ambient documentation — today's integration partner can become tomorrow's competitor (more on this below).

**6. Network effects.** The rarest and strongest — Chen's point is that Web 2.0's durable companies were "database wrappers" defended by networks, not technology. EvenUp's cross-firm settlement repository has a genuine multi-tenant flavor; most AI apps have none. Don't claim this moat unless customer N demonstrably makes the product better for customer N+1 through *interaction*, not just pooled data (which is moat #1, with all its diminishing-returns caveats).

**Sequencing under capital constraints.** These six are a menu only for the mega-funded. A pre-seed or seed founder cannot build six moats; realistically you can build one and a half. The standard sequence: start with **workflow lock-in (#3)** — it is the only moat buildable pre-revenue — plus **one deep integration (#2)** into the system of record your design partners already live in. Instrument outcome labels from day one so the **flywheel (#1)** can ignite later; it is a lagging moat. **Trust artifacts (#5)** accrue from the first compliance certification and lighthouse reference. **Network effects (#6)** and cross-tenant data pools require a customer base you will not have for years — never lead a seed pitch with them. **Distribution (#4)** is what the Series B buys.

### The Supplier Problem: Your Platform Is Also Your Competitor and Your Kill Switch

Even the best current counter-examples carry unresolved platform risk, and the Windsurf saga of 2025 is the purest demonstration. During OpenAI's pending ~$3B acquisition of Windsurf, Anthropic cut Windsurf's first-party access to Claude 3.5/3.7 Sonnet — co-founder Jared Kaplan: "it would be odd for us to be selling Claude to OpenAI." The OpenAI deal then collapsed on Microsoft IP objections; Google paid ~$2.4B to hire the CEO and key researchers; Cognition acquired the remainder — the endgame playing out within roughly 72 hours [Source: TechCrunch, June 3 / July 11 / July 14, 2025]. One company experienced its model supplier as competitor, its acquirer's rival, and its kill switch simultaneously.

Cursor, the strongest thick-wrapper success, now faces the same structure: Anthropic's Claude Code reached a reported ~$2.5B run-rate by early 2026, competing directly with Cursor while Cursor pays Anthropic retail for inference Anthropic gets at wholesale [Source: sacra.com/research/claude-code-vs-cursor; Anthropic Economic Index reporting]. This is not hypothetical; it is the current state of the largest application-layer category.

**Put numbers on it.** App-layer AI companies run blended gross margins of roughly 50–60%, versus 75–90% for traditional SaaS, with inference alone typically consuming ~20–23% of revenue — and, unlike SaaS hosting, that share tends to *grow* with usage [Source: Bessemer Feb 2026 pricing analysis via getmonetizely.com; saastr.com]. Worked example: at $100 of revenue with $25 of inference and $20 of other COGS, you have a 55% gross margin. If inference reprices 2x — a supplier decision entirely outside your control — you drop to 30%, which is uninvestable for a software multiple. Plan against a floor: target 60%+ blended gross margin at steady state, model the 2x-inference-shock case explicitly in your financial plan, and hold pricing power (usage-based or outcome-based components) that lets you pass through cost shocks.

**Open source is your margin lever — and your customer's exit.** The most important structural mitigation is the one Chen's 6-month-lag observation implies: route high-volume, low-differentiation paths (autocomplete, classification, extraction, first-draft generation) to open or self-hosted models, reserving frontier APIs for the hard 10% of calls. This is precisely what Cursor's in-house models do, and it is how you survive a repricing. But the same lever points at you: a large enterprise customer can eventually self-host an open model and rebuild your thin layers internally. The defense against that is everything in the taxonomy that isn't the model.

**The third adversary: domain incumbents.** Founders obsess over the labs and underweight a competitor that is often more dangerous — the incumbent who already owns the data and the distribution you're trying to earn. Thomson Reuters bought Casetext (CoCounsel) for $650M and sits on Westlaw; LexisNexis is both Harvey's partner and a competitor with Protégé; Elsevier and the journal publishers control the licenses OpenEvidence depends on; Epic is building native ambient documentation in the same chart Abridge writes to. The labs must build your domain assets from zero; the incumbents already have them and only need "good enough" AI — which, per Chen, open models hand them on a 6-month lag. Map this adversary explicitly, not as an aside.

### The Stress Test: Run This Before You Raise

Turn all of the above into a self-assessment. Answer in writing, pessimistically.

1. **The free-model test.** The next frontier release ships your core capability natively, free, tomorrow — DevDay 2023 for retrieval startups, ChatGPT for Jasper. What, specifically, do your customers still pay you for? If the answer is "our UX" or "our prompts," you are renting your moat from your supplier.
2. **The timing test (too early cuts too).** The mirror-image failure: the base model isn't good enough yet, and you burn capital on scaffolding — human review layers, elaborate correction pipelines — that subsidizes accuracy the next release will provide free. Ask: is my engineering spend building *durable assets* (integrations, data, workflow) or *compensating for model weakness*? The second category is a depreciating expense with roughly an 18-month half-life. If most of your headcount is compensation, you are early; being early is only good if your spend converts into assets that survive the model catching up.
3. **The data-flywheel audit.** Is your data proprietary, usage-generated, outcome-linked, and customer-visible — all four? Would a 10x-better base model substitute for it (Chegg's library: yes; EvenUp's settlements: no)? If any answer is wrong, you have data *scale*, not a flywheel, and it depreciates [Source: a16z]. And if you're pre-scale: are you capturing outcome labels from customer one?
4. **The rip-out test.** If a customer canceled today, what would they lose beyond the subscription — integrations, accumulated context, audit history, certified workflows? Estimate the switching cost in weeks of *their* labor. As an illustrative heuristic (not an empirical benchmark): under two weeks of replacement effort means a motivated buyer can churn inside one renewal conversation — that is thin.
5. **The supplier-conflict test.** Map every way your model provider could hurt you: entering your category (Claude Code), revoking access (Windsurf), repricing inference, or shipping your feature as a system prompt (Study Mode). What is your gross margin if inference prices double — is it still above ~40%? What share of calls could you route to open/self-hosted models within a quarter? What is your product if frontier access is cut?
6. **The incumbent test.** Separately from the labs: which domain incumbent (the Westlaw, the Epic, the Elsevier of your vertical) already owns the data and distribution you need, and what stops them from bolting "good enough" AI onto it? If your answer depends on incumbents being slow, remember they can buy speed (Casetext, $650M).
7. **The procurement test.** Can Microsoft or Google sell a "good enough" version through an existing enterprise agreement? If yes, is your vertical depth (compliance certifications, systems-of-record integration, domain trust) sufficient that "good enough" isn't? And have you budgeted the toll — $50–100K and 6–12 months for SOC 2 Type II, up to $150–500K and 9–24 months for HITRUST r2, 6–18 months for an Epic integration?
8. **The price-tier check.** Are you above the ~$250/month line where AI retention matches B2B SaaS, or in the sub-$50 tier where GRR runs ~23% [Source: ChartMogul]? Pricing into the upper tier legitimately means bundling workflow, seats, and outcomes a committee will defend at renewal — slapping a high price on a thin product just moves you into a tier whose buyers churn you with more paperwork.

Then keep measuring after you raise — a moat is a trend, not a checkbox. Track quarterly: **cohort NRR/GRR trend** (is each cohort retaining better than the last?); **switching-cost growth** (integrations live, accumulated context per account, weeks-of-labor to replace you); **non-model value share** (if you swapped your frontier model for an open one, how much measured outcome quality survives? — rising is a compounding moat, falling is erosion); and **flywheel crossover** (does pooled data measurably beat single-customer data yet?). A moat whose indicators are flat while the base model improves is a moat being eroded in place.

A defensible answer sheet looks like Abridge's or EvenUp's: value concentrated in assets a model release cannot reproduce — integrations, closed-loop proprietary data, regulatory trust — with the model treated as an improving, interchangeable input. An indefensible one looks like Jasper's October 2022 board deck.

Three final honesty notes for your own diligence. First, nearly every ARR figure in this chapter is self-reported by private companies and unaudited — YC publicly warned founders in April 2026 to "be truthful" about revenue claims, and AI-era "ARR" often includes volatile usage-based revenue [Source: Artificial Lawyer, April 2026]. Second, every thick exemplar here is mega-funded: Abridge ($5.3B), Harvey ($11B), EvenUp ($2B+), OpenEvidence ($12B) each raised hundreds of millions to buy their integrations, licenses, and data assets. If you are raising $2–5M, the honest version of this playbook is narrower: one workflow, one system-of-record integration, one compliance certification, outcome labels from day one — depth in a wedge small enough that the giants' indifference is itself your early moat. Third, survivorship cuts both ways: Cursor and Lovable prove wrappers can win, but their moats are years old at most and under active assault from their own suppliers. The correct posture is neither "wrappers die" nor "execution is everything." It is: the model is a rented input that will keep improving without you; build the company entirely out of the things that improve *because* of you.

---

## 3. Business Models and Monetization: Seats, Tokens, and Outcomes

### The cost floor moved, and the meter came with it

For twenty years, SaaS ran on a beautiful accounting accident: the marginal cost of one more user logging in was approximately zero. You built the software once, and every additional seat was almost pure gross margin. That is why classic SaaS businesses post 80–90% gross margins and why per-seat pricing became the industry's reflex [Source: bvp.com/atlas/the-ai-pricing-and-monetization-playbook].

LLM products break that accident. Every query a user sends incurs real, non-trivial compute. Cost scales with *usage*, not with logins. The consequence shows up directly on the income statement: AI-native gross margins run roughly 50–65%, not 85%. ICONIQ's January 2026 State of AI snapshot puts the average AI product gross margin at 52% — up from 41% in 2024 and ~45% in 2025, but still a full 30 points below traditional SaaS. Bessemer's 2025 read is blunter still: it pegs AI-company gross margins at 50–60% against SaaS's 80–90% [Sources: iconiq.com/growth/reports/2026-state-of-ai-bi-annual-snapshot; bvp.com/atlas/the-ai-pricing-and-monetization-playbook]. And inference is a *rising* share of that cost structure as you scale: ICONIQ's stage comparison shows pre-launch companies spending ~20% of total spend on model inference and ~32% on talent, while scaling companies spend ~23% on inference and ~26% on talent. The lesson isn't a calendar trend — it's that inference becomes a bigger slice of your P&L precisely as you grow into it.

If you price a usage-heavy product like it's zero-marginal-cost software, your heaviest, most-engaged users — the ones a SaaS founder is trained to celebrate — quietly become your biggest losses. That is the whole problem this chapter is about, and every monetization model below is a different answer to it.

### The four live models, and what each one implies

Four monetization patterns are in real production use in 2025–2026. Read them not as a menu of equally-good options but as a ladder of who carries the cost and reliability risk.

**Per-seat subscription.** You charge per user per month, usage bundled in. Buyers love it because it is predictable and legible to a finance team. It survives in AI in exactly one condition — and this condition is not "legacy," it is *correct* whenever it holds: when per-user usage is bounded and your COGS per seat is stable. A copilot with capped, low-COGS interactions should stay per-seat and not apologize for it. Salesforce's "digital labor" Agentforce licenses at $125/user/month are a per-seat framing of an agent [Source: salesforceben.com/huge-agentforce-pricing-shift-salesforce-introduces-pay-per-resolution]. The danger appears only when usage is unbounded: flat price, unbounded usage, and a marginal cost that isn't zero is a structural margin leak (the success penalty, below).

**Usage / consumption-based.** You charge per token, per API call, per action, or per credit. This is the technical-buyer default and the model the foundation labs themselves use. It puts cost risk squarely on the *buyer* — they pay for what they consume, so your margin is protected by construction. The cost is budget unpredictability, which enterprise finance teams hate. Salesforce's mid-2025 "Flex Credits" at roughly $0.10 per action is a consumption model [Source: salesforceben.com]. Bessemer frames this as the bottom rung of the value ladder: consumption-based, technical buyer, cost risk on the buyer [Source: bvp.com/atlas].

**Hybrid.** A base platform or subscription fee plus a usage or outcome tier on top, usually with safeguards — included allowances, minimum commitments, tiered overage rates. This is where the market has actually converged. ICONIQ's January 2026 data shows 49% of companies use annual commitments and 29% use tiered overage rates as guardrails [Source: saastr.com/the-execution-era-of-ai-5-key-takeaways-from-iconiqs-state-of-ai-report]. A clean example is **Zendesk**: its plans include a resolution allowance, and usage above it bills at a $2.00 pay-as-you-go rate — the subscription floors your revenue and bundles predictable volume, the overage protects margin at the top. **Agentforce** is a hybrid of the other shape: $125/user seats *plus* consumption via Flex Credits. The base fee buys predictability; the usage tier protects margin.

**Outcome / results-based.** You charge only when a defined result is delivered. This is the model everyone is talking about and the one that carries the most vendor risk. It aligns price perfectly with buyer value — but it transfers *all* the cost-of-serve and reliability risk onto you. Sierra, Intercom's Fin, Zendesk, and now Salesforce's Help Agent all sit here.

Adoption tells the honest story. ICONIQ's January 2026 numbers: subscription/platform 58%, usage-based 35%, outcome-based 18%. That 18% is a genuine surge — it was 2% in Q2 2025 — but it is still a minority [Source: saastr.com/the-execution-era-of-ai]. The named production deployments below are almost all enterprise contracts with heavy custom negotiation, which suggests (though ICONIQ doesn't break the 18% down this way) that outcome pricing is still concentrated at the top of the market, not defaulted to across SMB. Treat it as the frontier, not the norm.

### The concrete outcome-pricing examples, and what "an outcome" actually costs

The real 2025–2026 deployments are worth studying at the level of their fine print, because the fine print is the product.

**Intercom Fin** is the cleanest example of *pure* outcome pricing made safe by a minimum commitment — not a hybrid. It charges $0.99 per outcome with **no platform fee**, where an "outcome" is a Resolution, a Procedure handoff, or a Disqualification (each $0.99), or a Qualification ($9.99). Standalone Fin carries a 50-outcome-per-month minimum (~$49.50), which floors vendor revenue without bundling any included outcomes on top of a base fee — economically different from a hybrid's platform-fee-plus-overage. It bills once per conversation even if the agent takes many actions, and critically it *doesn't* bill the failure modes: rule-triggered escalations, procedure failures, unanswered clarifying questions, and customer-requested human handoffs are all free. A "resolution" requires Fin to have delivered an actual answer and the customer to confirm satisfaction or disengage (assumed after 24 hours of silence). Fin now runs standalone on top of Zendesk, Salesforce, HubSpot, Freshworks, and Zoho at the same $0.99, no seats required [Source: intercom.com/help/en/articles/8205718-fin-ai-agent-outcomes; getvoip.com/blog/fin-pricing]. Note the strategic significance: Intercom formally renamed itself Fin in May 2026, and in June 2026 Salesforce signed a definitive agreement to acquire it for approximately $3.6 billion — expected to close by early 2027 — a validation of the outcome-priced agent as a category [Source: salesforce.com/news/press-releases/2026/06/15/salesforce-signs-definitive-agreement-to-acquire-fin].

**Zendesk** was first in CX to go outcome-based, charging per "automated resolution" — $1.50 for committed-volume customers, $2.00 pay-as-you-go — with a resolution defined by a 72-hour quiet period during which the ticket stays closed [Source: zendesk.com/newsroom/articles/zendesk-outcome-based-pricing].

**Sierra** (Bret Taylor and Clay Bavor) runs outcome pricing at the enterprise tier: a pre-negotiated rate charged only when the agent resolves a case without human intervention; escalations are free. Sierra publishes no pricing, so the numbers in circulation are third-party estimates from vendor-comparison guides, not the company: annual contracts starting around $150,000, typical year-one budgets of $200,000–$350,000+, plus $50,000–$200,000 setup fees, with trivial interactions sometimes said to fall back to consumption billing (an unverified third-party description of private contracts) [Sources: fin.ai/learn; featurebase.app; eesel.ai — third-party estimates]. Bret Taylor's own framing — that buyers care about outcomes, not tokens — comes from his Cheeky Pint interview [Source: cheekypint.substack.com/p/bret-taylor-of-sierra-on-ai-agents]. Sierra reached ~$100M ARR in under two years and, by mid-2026, ~$200M ARR at a ~$15.8B valuation after a $950M Series E [Source: techcrunch.com/2025/11/21/bret-taylors-sierra-reaches-100m-arr-in-under-two-years]. It is the leading proof that outcome pricing scales — but it scales at six-figure contracts with bespoke negotiation over what counts.

**Salesforce** is the cautionary and instructive case: multiple pricing models in ~18 months. $2/conversation announced at Dreamforce in September 2024 (GA late October 2024), then ~$0.10/action Flex Credits (May 2025), then $125+/user seats, and in 2026 a pay-per-resolution Help Agent — announced June 25, 2026, GA July 2026 — that charges "a flat $2 when a resolution is achieved," nothing if the customer asks for a human or walks away unhappy [Sources: salesforceben.com; cmswire.com/contact-center/salesforce-debuts-help-agent-with-payperresolution-ai]. The original $2/conversation model backfired badly: Salesforce closed ~5,000 deals by early 2025 but only ~3,000 were paid, and the flat $2 "immediately priced out nonprofits, SMBs, and any organization without a large AI budget" [Source: saastr.com/salesforce-now-has-3-pricing-models-for-agentforce]. Salesforce could move to pay-per-resolution only *after* it had internal evidence — the Help Agent resolved 70% of 4.3 million inquiries on help.salesforce.com — that its resolution rate was high enough to make the model profitable. And even that flagship "pay per resolution" ships wrapped in a commit: resolutions are pre-purchased in blocks of **1,000** [Source: salesforceben.com; cio.com/article/4189183].

That sequencing is the lesson. Outcome pricing is a claim you can only make credibly once your evals prove your resolution rate — and even then you wrap it in a commitment.

### A worked example: what a $0.99 resolution actually earns you

The insurance framing below is only useful if you can put numbers to it. Here is a back-of-envelope model you can copy for a support-resolution agent priced at $0.99.

Assume prompt caching (on Anthropic-style caching, cache reads bill at ~0.1× the base input rate; OpenAI's automatic caching bills cached input at roughly 25–50% of the base input rate, depending on model) and model routing. The worked numbers below use the ~0.1× cache-read rate.

- **Easy case (password reset):** ~3 model calls, routed to a mid-tier model (Sonnet-class, ~$2–3/M input, ~$10–15/M output). Roughly 15K input tokens processed + ~1.5K output → **COGS ≈ $0.04–0.06.** Margin ≈ 94%.
- **Hard case (multi-step dispute):** ~12 calls, context growing toward ~40K tokens and re-sent each turn (cumulative ~250K input processed), on a frontier model (~$5/M input, ~$25/M output), ~8K output. Uncached that's ~$1.45; with caching on the repeated context, ~$0.40–0.60 → **COGS ≈ $0.40–1.45.** Margin ranges from +45% to *negative*.
- **The killer is retries.** You bill once per conversation, but a case that fails, retries, and then succeeds pays compute twice. A hard case that retries lands at ~$1.20–2.00 — underwater on a $0.99 price.

Now the blend, which is what actually determines profitability:

| Realized mix | Blended COGS | Margin on $0.99 |
|---|---|---|
| 80% easy / 20% hard | ~$0.33 | ~67% |
| 50% easy / 50% hard | ~$0.75 | ~24% |
| 50/50 with 15% retry rate | ~$0.95 | ~4% |

The break-even is sharp: **your $0.99 outcome goes underwater the moment hard-case share plus retry rate pushes blended COGS past $0.99.** That is roughly a 50/50 mix with resolution reliability below ~85%. Everything in the rest of this chapter — routing, caching, the reliability bar, the "quiet period" definitions — is in service of keeping the realized mix on the profitable side of that line.

### Outcome pricing is an insurance policy you are underwriting

The most useful mental model for outcome pricing is insurance. The price is the premium; the compute consumed is the claim. You are underwriting four distinct risks [Source: thepricingconundrum.substack.com/p/outcome-based-pricing-as-insurance]:

1. **Variance in cost-to-serve.** A single $0.99 resolution might cost you $0.05 or ~$1.45. Your profitability depends entirely on the *realized mix* matching your *modeled mix* — exactly the table above.
2. **Adverse selection.** Healthy companies with clean data prefer flat pricing. High-cost, messy-workflow firms gravitate toward pay-for-performance precisely because it shifts their mess onto you. The buyers most attracted to your outcome pricing are systematically the ones who make it unprofitable.
3. **Moral hazard.** Once a customer pays only for outcomes, they stop maintaining their knowledge base and cleaning their data — the complexity is now your problem, and your cost-to-serve drifts up.
4. **Correlated losses.** An outage or a bad release makes thousands of end-users contact the agent about the same hard problem *simultaneously*, spiking cost across your entire portfolio at the worst possible moment — the insurer's "many houses burning at once."

Safe operation requires the same tools an actual insurer uses: scale and diversification, risk-based (personalized) pricing, active cost-routing of easy queries to cheaper models, and hybrid caps — platform fees, allowances, minimum commitments. This is why almost nobody runs *pure* outcome pricing without a floor; even Fin wraps its $0.99 in a 50-outcome minimum.

### Pricing against an input cost that mostly falls — but not monotonically

Your dominant input cost — inference — is deflating faster than almost any input in business history. Epoch AI found the price to match GPT-4's performance on PhD-level science (GPQA) fell ~40x per year; across benchmarks, decline rates ranged 9x–900x/year, median ~50x/year, accelerating to a median ~200x/year when you exclude pre-2024 data. Epoch's own caveat is worth quoting: these extreme recent declines *may not persist* — it makes no forward projection beyond that [Source: epoch.ai/data-insights/llm-inference-price-trends]. (My own guess is that the ~10x/year rate moderates over time; treat that as speculation, not Epoch's data.)

But "prices fall" is not the same as "prices only fall," and the counterexample is instructive. Claude Sonnet 5 launched at an *introductory* $2/$10 per million tokens — scheduled to rise to $3/$15 after August 31, 2026 — and its new tokenizer maps the same input to roughly 1.0–1.35× more tokens [Source: Anthropic model documentation]. So a workload can see its effective per-request cost on a frontier model *go up* at a version boundary even as the broad curve falls. This is exactly why you must not bake a specific cost curve into a contract.

The defensible posture:

- **Price to value, not to cost.** Bessemer's advice is blunt: "Start with a price. If customers say sold immediately, you're too cheap." Raise until you hear "we have to think about that" [Source: bvp.com/atlas]. If your price is anchored to buyer ROI, falling inference cost becomes expanding margin, not a repricing liability.
- **Harvest the deflation as margin, not as price cuts you're forced into.** The near-term levers are within your control: prompt caching (Anthropic cache reads bill at ~10% of the base input rate; OpenAI's cached input bills at 25–50% of base — a 50–75% discount depending on model [Sources: openai.com/index/api-prompt-caching; developers.openai.com/api/docs/pricing]), batch processing (~50% off), and model routing — easy queries to cheap models, frontier models reserved for hard ones.
- **Keep pricing decoupled from token count** where you can, so you never renegotiate every time a model gets cheaper — or every time a new frontier model launches more expensive. Outcome and credit pricing do this automatically.

### Credit-based pricing: the packaging compromise everyone converged on

Between raw tokens and pure outcomes sits the packaging pattern that quietly dominates 2025–2026: **abstracted credits.** You define an internal unit ("a credit"), price actions in credits, and sell credits in packs or commitments. Salesforce Flex Credits and Cursor's usage credits are both this. The appeal is structural: credits *decouple the price metric from raw tokens*, so you can re-rate what a credit buys as model costs fall without touching the customer's contract; they smooth model-mix changes; and they slot naturally into pre-purchase commits. The downside is the one that burned Cursor (next section): **opacity.** When a customer can't see how fast a credit burns or which model a request routed to, a credit balance becomes a landmine. If you use credits, make the conversion visible and stable, and show a live burn-down. Credits are a re-rating tool, not a fog machine.

### The success penalty, and how not to get killed by it

Under flat-rate pricing, if your heaviest user costs 100x more to serve than your lightest, "flat subscription pricing mixes margins in a way that's hard to sustain" [Source: softwarepricing.com/blog/saas-pricing-models]. Your best customers subsidize your worst, then your best customers churn to a competitor who priced their light usage fairly, and you're left with a book of unprofitable heavy users. That is the success penalty, and it is exactly what nearly killed Cursor.

Cursor's $20/month Pro plan bundled generous frontier-model access, betting developer LTV would exceed per-request costs. ARR went from near-zero to $100M+ in twelve months — and then per-request costs climbed. The mechanism matters for this chapter's thesis: per-*token* list prices mostly fell, but per-*request* cost rose because agentic coding uses long context and many multi-step calls, and because premium models (Claude Opus–class) are expensive per request. On June 16, 2025 Cursor shifted to a usage-credit model; power users doing large refactors hit surprise overages, including a widely-shared ~$7,225 invoice, and reports of an entire monthly allocation burning in a few heavy prompts. CEO Michael Truell published the company's public apology — the "Clarifying Our Pricing" post — on July 4, 2025 (trade-press coverage followed over the next few days, e.g. TechCrunch on July 7), offered refunds for surprise usage between June 16 and July 4, and restructured [Sources: cursor.com/blog/june-2025-pricing; techcrunch.com/2025/07/07/cursor-apologizes-for-unclear-pricing-changes-that-upset-users; finout.io/blog/what-happened-to-cursor-pricing-2026-guide].

The defenses are all forms of *reconnecting price to marginal cost* — but the fatal error wasn't the economics, it was the opacity. Cursor's silent change, the invisible routing to expensive models, and the ambiguous "unlimited" did the damage. Operationalize the defenses with numbers, not slogans:

- **Track per-account gross margin weekly.** Alert when any account's monthly COGS exceeds, say, 70% of its ARPU — those are your future losses, visible now.
- **Ship rate limits, fair-use policies, and hard spend caps from day one.** Every durable flat-rate AI product has them; they are the cheapest defense against the success penalty and belong in the pre-launch checklist alongside metering.
- **Show users a live usage meter.** The concrete lesson from Cursor: make consumption visible before you make it billable. "Pricing is product," and repeatedly walking back changes trains customers that your pricing is negotiable — its own long-term tax.

### Free tiers and trials have real COGS now

One more thing that hits a founder *before* any outcome-pricing question: in AI products, free users cost real money. The classic PLG playbook — a generous free tier to drive adoption — assumes near-zero marginal cost, which no longer holds. Rework it: cap free usage in credits rather than time, route free-tier traffic to cheaper models only, and time-box trials. A free tier that silently runs frontier inference is a subsidy you can't see until the bill arrives.

### Before you launch: metering, budgeting, and the definition of "done"

Three pieces of infrastructure have to exist *before* you turn on usage or outcome pricing.

**Metering and billing — buy, don't build.** You need accurate per-event capture, real-time aggregation, contract-aware invoicing, spend caps, and dispute-ready outcome logs.
