2. **What is your wedge?** Not a category ("legal AI") but a specific, painful, frequent workflow owned by an identifiable buyer with budget. Cursor's wedge was the editor itself; Harvey's was elite-firm legal work. Narrow beats broad at entry, every time.
3. **Is the demand real or reflected hype?** Distinguish "our board says we need an AI strategy" budgets from "we will churn if you take this away" usage. Only the second survives 2026.
4. **Which of the six moat types do you credibly build within 18 months?** Proprietary data flywheel, system-of-record integration depth, workflow lock-in, distribution, regulated-domain trust, or switching costs. If your honest answer is "prompt quality and UX polish," you are renting your moat from your model vendor.
5. **Model-improvement stress test:** assume the next frontier release does your core task 10x better out of the box. Write down what happens to your product. If the answer is "we die," stop. If the answer is "our costs drop and our data/workflow position deepens," proceed.

### Gate 2: The Economics Test

Run the numbers before you fall in love. The margin structure of AI-native software is genuinely different — 50-60% gross margins per Bessemer and ICONIQ 2026 data, versus 80-90% for classic SaaS — and it is survivable only if you engineer for it.

- **Model cost per completed task**, not per API call. Agentic loops consume 5-30x the tokens of a single chat completion; model your actual workflow, including retries and failures.
- **Price against value, cost against reality.** Choose your pricing model deliberately: seats for tool-like products, usage for variable workloads, outcomes (the Intercom Fin ~$0.99-per-resolution pattern, Sierra-style contracts) only if your reliability and eval discipline can absorb the risk you are taking on. Outcome pricing is a margin bet on your own quality bar.
- **Identify your routing lever.** Multi-model routing — frontier models for hard steps, cheap models for easy ones — is the single biggest COGS lever you control. If your architecture cannot route, redesign it now, not at Series A.
- **Default-alive math:** at your target price and realistic token consumption, does a customer generate positive gross margin in month one? If profitability depends entirely on future inference-price declines, you have a subsidized demo, not a business.

### Gate 3: The Reliability Test

Evals are the new PRD. Before launch, you should be able to answer: what is your task success rate, measured on a held-out set of real customer scenarios, and what happens on failure?

- Build the eval suite **before** the product hardens — it is your spec, your regression net, and your enterprise sales collateral.
- Instrument tracing and observability from day one; you cannot fix agent behavior you cannot see.
- Design human-in-the-loop escalation as a product feature, not an apology. Unclear value and inadequate controls are the leading causes of the projected 40%+ agentic-project cancellations — controls *are* the product in enterprise contexts.
- If you plan outcome pricing, your eval discipline is your underwriting function. Treat it that way.

### Gate 4: The Distribution and Compliance Test

- Name your first ten customers — actual companies or people, not personas — and how you reach them without paid acquisition.
- Recruit 3-5 design partners who commit real workflow data and weekly feedback in exchange for pricing and influence. If you cannot recruit them, the market is telling you something.
- Decide PLG versus enterprise sales based on your buyer's procurement reality, not your preference.
- Run the regulatory screen: if you touch the EU, know where you fall under the AI Act's phased regime (high-risk obligations apply from August 2026, fines up to 7% of global turnover). In regulated verticals, treat compliance as a moat to build early, not a tax to defer.

### The First-12-Months Sequence

If you clear the four gates, execute in this order — the sequence matters because each step de-risks the next:

1. **Months 0-2:** Validate the thesis with 20+ practitioner interviews in your wedge; sign design partners; write the eval spec for the core task.
2. **Months 2-5:** Ship a reliability-instrumented MVP to design partners only. Measure task success rate, cost per task, and time-to-value. Do not launch publicly.
3. **Months 5-8:** Run pricing experiments across at least two models (e.g., hybrid seat-plus-usage versus per-outcome). Track gross margin per customer weekly.
4. **Months 8-12:** Convert design partners to paid; establish the metrics a seed raise now requires — genuine retention and margin data, not the inflated "record ARR" that pervades seed-stage AI. Raise against milestones you control, remembering that 25-30x revenue multiples price in expectations you will have to meet.

### The Honest Close

This book has deliberately spent more pages on failure modes than on upside, because the upside needs no advertising — over 60% of venture dollars already chase it. The founders who win in this space are not the most excited about AI; they are the most clear-eyed about what they own versus what they borrow. You are building on infrastructure that improves monthly, is priced by someone else, and is available to every competitor. Your business is whatever remains after you subtract the model: the data only you accumulate, the workflow only you sit inside, the trust only you have earned, the distribution only you command.

If the checklist above says go — go fast, because category windows in this market close in quarters, not years. If it says no, that conclusion is worth exactly as much: the discipline to not build the wrong company is the rarest founder skill of the current cycle. Either way, decide on evidence. That was the point of the book.

---
